The Ultimate SOP for Creating Killer Ads
By Zakaria_Airakaz

1.

🧠 The Mission & Philosophy of Killer Ads

The purpose of a high-converting ad is not to convince — it’s to align belief and trigger
resonance.
A great ad:
●​ Captures attention through emotional relevance​
●​ Builds belief through logic and story​
●​ Makes conversion feel inevitable and self-driven​

Ads are belief engineering systems.​
They are not designed to push a product — they are built to resolve emotional tension in your
avatar’s life.

📚

2.
Core Psychological Frameworks That Drive Ad
Success
Below are the deepest psychological levers used to construct and diagnose ad performance
across all formats (UGC, VSLs, Static).

2.1 System 1 & 2 Thinking – Daniel Kahneman
This foundational framework explains how the brain makes decisions, and it's critical for
structuring winning ads. ( I already i explained this one on the LP SOP here is a reminder)
Note: This principle is crucial because it aligns with how humans naturally think and make
decisions. The key is understanding which system — emotional (System 1) or rational (System

2) — dominates in your specific avatar. Only then can you craft messaging that truly resonates
and drives action.​
​
EG:​
​

System 1-Dominant Avatar
Example: Woman dealing with PCOS-related hair loss
●​ Feels hopeless, ashamed, and emotionally drained from failed fixes​
●​ Makes decisions based on how a story feels, not what it proves​
●​ Needs to feel understood, not educated

System 2-Dominant Avatar
Example: 40+ German Mfer man with chronic back pain
●​ Logical, skeptical, wants clinical proof and long-term relief​
●​ Compares options, reads reviews, checks mechanisms​
●​ Needs explanation before belief
System 1: The Fast, Feeling Brain
●​ Instinctive, subconscious, emotional, fast.
●​ Processes visuals, emotions, patterns, and stories in milliseconds.
●​ It decides before the conscious brain catches up.

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Your hook must target System 1.
●​ Use facial expressions, contrast, emotional mirroring, and pattern interruption.
●​ Visuals > words. Stories > stats. Emotion > explanation.

💬 Example:

●​ Showing a woman crying in front of a mirror (System 1) will outperform a line that says
"Improve your skin clarity" (System 2).
System 2: The Slow, Thinking Brain
●​ Analytical, conscious, logical, effortful.
●​ Kicks in after interest is sparked.

●​ It justifies, compares, evaluates.

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Your ad must shift gears after the emotional hook.
●​ Present your unique mechanism, customer proof, scientific backing.
●​ This is where the belief is confirmed.

💬 Example:

●​ After showing the emotional struggle, explain: "This device uses red light at a
wavelength of 660nm, clinically shown to increase collagen by 31% in 8 weeks."

🧠 High-performing ads work because they mimic how the brain works:
1.​ System 1: "This feels like me. I’m in."
2.​ System 2: "This makes sense. I trust it."

If you skip System 1: You never get attention. If you skip System 2: You never get conversion.

➡️ Structure your ads like this (System 1 → System 2 flow):
●​ 0–3s: Visual & emotional hook (System 1)​

●​ 4–10s: Avatar pain + agitation of the problem (System 1)​
●​ 11–18s: Mechanism, proof, and logical solution (System 2)​
●​ 19–24s: Paint the desire — what life looks like after using the product (System 2 +
emotional closure)​
●​ 25s+: Clear, low-friction, benefit-driven CTA
The secret to high ROAS isn’t better logic — it’s starting with better emotion.

2.2 COM-B Model – Behavior Change Science
The COM-B model explains what’s truly required for someone to take action. It stands for:
●​ Capability – “Can I do this?”
●​ Opportunity – “Is this possible for me right now?”
●​ Motivation – “Do I even want this badly enough?”
Without all three present, action won't happen.

🔧 Capability (C)
The person must believe they’re capable of achieving the result.
●​ Is the product easy to use?
●​ Does it feel like something they can actually do?

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Show the product in use by someone like them
●​ Use language like: “It’s as simple as...” or “Anyone can follow this.”

💬 Example:

●​ “Even if you’ve failed 5 times before — this takes 90 seconds a day.”

🛠 Opportunity (O)

They must have the external chance to do it.
●​ Is the product available?
●​ Is it affordable? Can they get it in their country? Is it in stock?

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Show clear pricing, shipping info, availability
●​ Use phrases like: “Ships today,” “In stock now,” “Under $1/day.”

💬 Example:

●​ “Delivered to your door. No prescription needed.”

🔥 Motivation (M)

They must truly desire the outcome your product provides.
●​ Is the emotional or functional result powerful enough?
●​ Are they personally invested in fixing it?

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Mirror their internal frustration or goal
●​ Paint a clear before/after transformation
●​ Use emotional language: “You deserve to feel like yourself again.”

💬 Example:

●​ “You’ve tried everything — but this time, the results stay.”

🧠 Use the COM-B Model to diagnose friction:
●​ If an ad isn’t converting, ask:
○​ Do they believe they can do this? (C)
○​ Is it clearly available to them? (O)
○​ Is the desire powerful and emotional enough? (M)

✅ The best ads remove friction in all 3 — making action feel natural, obvious, and urgent.
2.3 Self-Determination Theory ( This is the reason I keep saying stick to one avatar and
try to know him the best )
This theory explains the 3 fundamental psychological needs that must be satisfied for a person
to feel empowered enough to act — especially when the action involves change, purchase, or
commitment.

⚙️ Autonomy – "I choose this myself"

People resist being sold to. They want to feel like the decision was theirs — not the result of
pressure.

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Let the viewer feel in control
●​ Use copy that invites rather than pushes
●​ Avoid heavy-handed CTAs

💬 Example:

●​ “Here’s what finally worked for me. You decide if it’s right for you.”

💪 Competence – "I can actually do this"

If something feels too hard, confusing, or uncertain — people avoid it. Belief in their ability to
succeed is essential.

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Use simple demos or testimonials showing success by people like them
●​ Reduce perceived effort or learning curve

💬 Example:

●​ “I was skeptical too — but all I had to do was use it for 3 minutes a day.”

🤝 Relatedness – "This is made for someone like me"
People want to feel seen and understood. They’re far more likely to take action when they feel
the brand “gets” their situation.

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Feature UGC or testimonials from relatable avatars
●​ Use language and visuals that reflect their real world

💬 Example:

●​ “I’m a mom of 3 — this was the first thing that actually fit my routine.”

🧠 High-converting ads satisfy all 3:

●​ Autonomy: Viewer feels in control, not pushed
●​ Competence: Viewer believes they can succeed
●​ Relatedness: Viewer feels emotionally safe and understood

➡️ When these 3 align, resistance disappears — and the viewer moves forward because they
want to, not because they’re being sold.

2.4 Narrative Transportation Theory ( this why I like VSLs or UGC vsl style stories
always dominate)
We act when we’re emotionally transported into a story — not just shown a product.
Stories create immersion. When a viewer gets pulled into a narrative, they temporarily suspend
skepticism and emotionally live the transformation.
This is called "narrative transportation." And it creates a high-trust, low-resistance
state.

🧠 Why it works:

●​ The brain processes stories like experiences, not facts.
●​ When someone watches a relatable transformation, mirror neurons activate — making
them feel like it's happening to them. And Mfers if it is done right, this creates a
trance-like emotional state where the prospect doesn’t just want the result… they feel
like they’ve already experienced it. And that emotional imprint drives a powerful urge to
take action and own the product.

●​ This emotional engagement increases attention span, memory retention, and
conversion.

✅ Great ads are micro-stories — not pitches.
Structure of a Story-Driven Ad:

1.​ Problem: Start with the avatar's emotional pain or frustration.
2.​ Tension: Show the struggle, the breaking point, or the moment of doubt.
3.​ Solution: Introduce the product naturally — not as a savior, but as the enabler of
change.
4.​ Resolution: Paint what life looks like after — the relief, pride, control, or peace.

💬 Example:

●​ “I used to dread leaving the house. Now, I go out feeling confident again.”

✅ Story-driven ads work best with:
●​ Raw UGCs
●​ Hero story VSLs

🎯 Use stories that reflect your viewer’s starting point — and take them through a believable,
emotionally satisfying transformation.

Because at the end of the day, people don’t remember what you told them. They remember how
you made them feel.

2.5 Loss Aversion (Behavioral Economics)
Loss Aversion is one of the most proven psychological principles in behavioral economics. It
shows that people feel the pain of a loss twice as strongly as the pleasure of a gain.
In other words, your prospect is more likely to take action to avoid something bad
than to chase something good.
This principle flips the default persuasion strategy. Instead of only saying “here’s what you’ll
get,” you must emphasize, “here’s what you’re losing if you don’t act.”

🧠 Why it works:

●​ The human brain is wired for survival. Avoiding loss feels urgent. Again Mfers most us
don’t change our life chasing a dream. We change because not changing becomes too
painful. When there’s too much to lose, fear takes the wheel — and that’s what drives

action. It’s not the outcome that pushes us— it’s the cost of staying the same.​
●​ Loss-based framing taps into fear, anxiety, and discomfort — which are powerful
motivators for decision-making.

✅ Great ads don’t just highlight benefits — they shine a spotlight on the cost of doing nothing.
💬 Example:
●​ “Every day you wait, your hair keeps thinning — and your beauty keeps slipping.”
●​ “Ignore the back pain, and in six months, you’ll be looking at surgery instead of relief.”

🔥 How to apply Loss Aversion in Ads:

●​ Show what they’re currently losing (health, time, joy, confidence)
●​ Frame the problem as getting worse with inaction
●​ Make “waiting” feel like the dangerous option

💥 CTA Twist: Instead of:

●​ “Buy now to feel better” Use:
●​ “Stop letting this steal your energy and confidence”

✅ When your ad makes the viewer feel like they’re already losing, the urgency to act becomes
natural — and conversion follows.

2.6 Anticipated Regret
Anticipated regret is a powerful psychological driver — rooted in our fear of looking back and
wishing we had acted sooner.
People are often more motivated to avoid future guilt than to chase current gain.
The emotional pain of “What if I had...” can be so vivid, it compels action now.

✅ Great ads activate this future pain in the present — turning hesitation into urgency.
🧠 Why it works:

●​ We visualize our future self regretting inaction more vividly than enjoying success.
●​ Fear of missing the window to act creates real-time tension.
●​ This works especially well for health, appearance, opportunity, and time-sensitive offers.

💬 Example angles:

●​ “You’ll wish you had started 3 months ago.”
●​ “This might be your last shot before it gets worse.”
●​ “Fix it now, or fix something bigger later.”

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Use future pacing: “Imagine where you’ll be 6 months from now…”
●​ Layer testimonials that express regret: “I wish I hadn’t waited so long to try this.”
●​ Include soft deadline pressure: “Thousands have already started — what’s stopping
you?”

✅ When your ad plants the seed of future disappointment, you create urgency that feels

internal — not forced.​
​
​
I know some of you Mfers will get confused What is the difference between Loss Aversion and
Anticipated Regret:
●​ Loss Aversion = “I don’t want to lose what I have now.”​
○​ Focus: Present pain or risk
○​ Trigger: Fear of losing something you already possess (health, time, money,
confidence)
○​ Emotional Driver: Avoiding a current or ongoing loss​
●​ Anticipated Regret = “I’ll regret it later if I don’t act now.”​
○​ Focus: Future guilt or missed opportunity
○​ Trigger: Fear of looking back and wishing they made a different choice
○​ Emotional Driver: Avoiding future pain or what-if
​

2.7 Mirror Neurons & Identification
Mirror neurons are brain cells that fire not only when we do something — but also when we
observe someone else doing it. This is why we can feel pain, joy, or relief just by watching
someone go through it — our brain is mirroring the emotional experience.
In advertising, this means if your viewer watches someone like them win, they start
to emotionally experience that win too.

This creates belief, emotional resonance, and a surge in motivation — because it stops being
their story and becomes mine.

🧠 Why this matters:

When your ad reflects your audience’s reality — their fears, hopes, frustrations, language — it
dissolves resistance and builds trust fast. They’re not watching an ad. They’re watching
themselves.

✅ Ads must show avatars that reflect your audience’s:

●​ Life stage — age, gender, daily role (e.g., young mom, working dad, retiree)
●​ Emotional pain — shame, fear, doubt, confusion, regret
●​ Social context — how they live, speak, behave, dress, scroll, interact

💬 Example:

●​ “I’m a 32-year-old mom who’s been battling PCOS for years — and I was terrified of
losing more hair… until I tried this.”

🔁 In Ads:

●​ Use real customers or relatable actors with the same body type, language, lifestyle
●​ Prioritize native settings: a bathroom mirror, the car, the living room
●​ Let them see themselves in the outcome
Because when a viewer sees someone like them transform, the brain doesn’t say: “I want that.”
It says: “That’s possible for me too.”
I know some of you Mfers will get confused What is the difference between Mirror Neurons &
Identification and Self-Determination Theory:
●​ Mirror Neurons & Identification = “That’s me.”​
○​
○​
○​
○​

Mechanism: Neurological mirroring
Trigger: Watching someone like us succeed or struggle
Effect: We feel their experience as if it's ours
Purpose in ads: Create emotional resonance and visual recognition​

●​ Self-Determination Theory = “I want this because I choose it.”
○​ Mechanism: Psychological motivation model
○​ 3 Core Needs:
■​ Autonomy – “I choose this freely”
■​ Competence – “I believe I can do this”
■​ Relatedness – “This fits someone like me”
○​ Effect: The viewer feels emotionally safe, capable, and motivated

○​ Purpose in ads: Reduce internal resistance and trigger self-motivated action

2.8 Processing Fluency
Processing Fluency is the psychological principle that explains how the brain favors ease. When
something is easy to see, read, or understand, it feels more true, trustworthy, and desirable.
If it takes effort to understand, the brain interprets it as risky, confusing, or
untrustworthy.
This applies to both the visual structure and verbal delivery in your ads.

🧠 Why this matters in advertising:

●​ The average viewer makes a judgment in under 1 second
●​ Mental effort = friction, and friction kills conversions
●​ When your ad flows smoothly, the brain says: “This makes sense.” And this is your
biggest issue MFERS this time MFERS with capital
— you write your ads in pieces.
You come up with the hook, then go scroll on Instagram, talk on Discord, and later come
back to slap the rest of it together. Then you send it over like, “Yo, can you review this?”
Bro, you're killing my brain cells. The flow’s completely off because you didn’t write it in
one focused block. You didn’t sit down and stay in the zone to make sure it reads
smooth, front to back. At the very least, you need to set time to write in one shot — or
give it a clean pass to ensure it actually feels like one cohesive message.

😡

✅ High-converting ads feel simple and intuitive — even if the message is deep
Visual Processing Fluency:

●​ Avoid visual clutter (too much text, chaotic backgrounds): Now I know you’re already
thinking, “But what about those f*cking TikTok ads with a million cuts and chaos
everywhere? read this Mfers with my trolling voice:
Those TikTok-style ads with fast transitions, flashy B-rolls, and jump cuts do work — but
not because they’re chaotic. They work because they’re designed for attention-deficit
environments where every second is a battle to stay on screen.
But here’s what most people miss:

✅ The best of those “chaotic” ads still have fluency underneath the noise.
●​
●​
●​
●​

Each scene has one clear message.
Text is bold and bite-sized.
Visuals follow a rhythm — even if it’s fast.
Every transition is designed to reset attention without confusing the viewer.

So it’s not about clutter — it’s about controlled chaos with clarity at its core.
Think of it like this:
It’s loud, fast, and aggressive — but it’s intentional and fluent.​
If your ad feels like a jumbled mess or jumps topic mid-frame — it’s not a
TikTok-style win, it’s just trash and if you keep sending me trash I will leak
your store and make people make fun of you so level your game up Mfers​

●​ Use contrast and white space to guide the eye
●​ Stick to one focal point per frame

💬 Example:

●​ Before: 3 text blocks, no spacing, no hierarchy
●​ After: One bold headline, one line of benefit, clear CTA
Verbal Processing Fluency:
●​ Use short words, short sentences
●​ Avoid jargon or abstract metaphors
●​ Speak like the customer speaks

💬 Example:

●​ Instead of: “A revolutionary, full-spectrum, biophotonic rejuvenation system…”
●​ Say: “Red light that helps your skin look younger — fast.”

✅ Keep your ads clear, clean, and conversational. If a 12-year-old can’t understand it in 3
seconds, simplify it.

Because in ads, confusion doesn’t cause curiosity — it causes exits.

2.9 Anchoring
Anchoring is a pricing psychology principle that shows how our brains don’t evaluate price in
isolation — we compare it to the first number we see.

That first number becomes the "anchor," and it sets the baseline for what we think
something should cost.

⚠️ Important Distinction:

Anchoring is primarily an offer strategy, not a top-of-funnel ad tactic. But it can still be very
effective inside ads when used at the right time.

✅ Best used in ads when:

●​ You're retargeting warm traffic or cart abandoners basically MOF/BOF
●​ You're running a longer VSL where you frame price/value at the end
●​ You're using UGC where the buyer talks about how surprisingly affordable it was

🚫 Not ideal for cold ads: TOF

●​ Anchoring too early can break fluency and feel like a hard pitch
●​ Most cold viewers aren’t ready to hear about price — they need belief and emotion first

🧠 Why Anchoring Works:
●​ Our brains need a reference point to decide what’s “expensive” or “affordable”
●​ The initial number shown influences everything after it — even if it's irrelevant

✅ In the right kind of ad, use:

●​ A higher price first, then reveal your actual offer
●​ Side-by-side comparisons or slashed pricing

💬 Examples:

●​ “Normally $299 — today only $79.”​
●​ “Most people spend over $1,200 on treatments that barely touch the root cause. This
does more — for less than a week’s worth of takeout.”​
●​ “A single Botox session costs $350 — and wears off in months. This helps rebuild your
collagen naturally for under $2/day. No needles. No regret.”​
●​ “Physical therapy: $85/session. Massage: $120/hr. Back surgery? Over $18,000.This?
One-time $179 — and it sits in your drawer, ready whenever you need relief.”

🎯 Anchoring can also apply to value perception:

●​ “10+ hours of relief in one 3-minute session.”
●​ “$1/day to finally sleep through the night.”

✅ Use it carefully and strategically — anchoring works best when your viewer is already sold
emotionally and just needs a logical reason to act now. that shows how our brains don’t
evaluate price in isolation — we compare it to the first number we see.

That first number becomes the "anchor," and it sets the baseline for what we think
something should cost.
This is why luxury brands always show the highest price first — and why discounts feel way
more powerful when we see what we’re “saving” versus what we’re paying.

2.10 The Pratfall Effect
The Pratfall Effect is a psychological principle showing that people actually trust and like you
more when you show a small, harmless flaw — as long as you’re otherwise competent. In
advertising, this means imperfection isn’t a liability. It’s a tool. This principle is also what makes
ugly ads work so well. When you see a content creator filming in a messy house, with regular
lighting, home clothes, unbrushed hair — it hits different. It’s not a promotion. It’s not polished.
It’s real. You’re watching a normal human being who’s showing up raw, flaws and all — and
that’s exactly why we trust them.
A little mess makes your message feel real.
When your ads are too polished or overly scripted, they lose believability. But when they show
human quirks — like a stutter, a laugh, or a slightly chaotic background — they feel authentic
and relatable. Viewers don’t just watch, they lean in.

🧠 Why this works:

●​ Perfection triggers skepticism
●​ Flaws feel honest, and honesty builds trust
●​ Imperfection lowers resistance by mirroring real life

✅ In Ads:

●​ Show unfiltered UGCs with messy hair, pauses, or raw moments
●​ Keep background noise, small mistakes, and casual delivery
●​ Avoid overediting or stiff scripting — let the human moments stay

💬 Example:

●​ “Okay, ignore how tired I look — I’ve been using this for 3 weeks and it’s actually
helping.”
●​ Laughs mid-sentence “Wow I sound like an infomercial, but it really works.”

✅ Ads that use the Pratfall Effect feel like a recommendation from a friend — not a pitch from a
brand. That’s why they convert.

🎯 Don’t aim for perfection. Aim for connection.
2.11 The Zeigarnik Effect
The Zeigarnik Effect is the psychological tendency to remember unfinished tasks or
incomplete stories better than completed ones. In advertising, this becomes a powerful tool for
holding attention — because the brain hates unresolved tension.
If something starts but doesn’t finish, the mind stays hooked until it gets closure.​
​
This is what I call the knowledge gap. Always create a gap in your viewer’s mind —
a space between what they know and what they need to know. That gap pulls
attention like a magnet and leads to longer watch time, every single time.

✅ That’s why your hook should open a loop — a question, a cliffhanger, or a surprising
statement that demands resolution.

🧠 Why it works:

●​ Incomplete information creates mental tension
●​ Curiosity becomes a discomfort the viewer wants to resolve
●​ The brain stays engaged to close the loop

💬 Example Hooks:
●​

PROBLEM-AWARE :
○​ “I thought my hormones were the problem… but the real reason my hair kept
falling out was way worse.”​
“Every shower, I’d lose more — until one blood result finally explained
everything.”
○​ “I tried biotin, oils, scalp brushes… none of it worked. And the worst part? I found
out why way too late.”​
​
​

●​ SOLUTION-AWARE:
○​ “I spent $600 on supplements before realizing the one hormone no one talks
about was ruining my progress.”
○​ “It wasn’t a deficiency — it was something my doctor missed entirely.”
○​ “I thought I was doing everything right… until I tracked this one thing for a week.”

✅ In Ads:
●​ Don’t give away the result in the first frame
●​ Build story or curiosity before solution
●​ Let the first 5–10 seconds stir the question, not the pitch

🎯 Good Zeigarnik-style ads create an emotional itch the viewer has to scratch — and the only
way to do that is to watch until the end.

2.12 Social Proof & Bandwagon Bias
Social Proof is the psychological principle that we look to others when we’re unsure what to do
— and Bandwagon Bias says the more people we see doing something, the more likely we are
to join in.
When we don’t fully trust ourselves, we trust the crowd.
In advertising, this means showing that your product is already loved, trusted, and used by
others instantly builds trust and reduces buying resistance.

🧠 Why it works:

●​ Makes the viewer feel they’re not taking a risk​
●​ Activates “safety in numbers”​
●​ Creates FOMO (Fear of Missing Out) when everyone else seems to be using it​

✅ How to apply in ads:
●​ Use review screenshots, quick montages of testimonials, or “reaction” duet content​
●​ Highlight big metrics:​

○​ “Over 1 million sold”​
○​ “4.9 stars from 30,000+ customers”​
○​ “Trusted by 8,000 women with PCOS”​
●​ Include comments, shares, or clips with people saying, “I saw this everywhere…”​

💬 Example lines:
●​ “I wasn’t sure at first, but with over 12,000 five-star reviews, I gave it a shot.”​
●​ “Everyone I know is trying this — so I finally did too.”​
●​ “It showed up in three of my group chats… that’s when I knew I had to try it.”​

🎯 You’re not just selling a product. You’re selling the comfort of joining a movement that
already makes sense to everyone else.

2.13 The Psychology of Dopamine in Ads
Dopamine is the brain’s chemical of anticipation and reward — and it plays a massive role in
how viewers respond to your ad.
It’s not released when someone gets something — it spikes when they expect
something exciting, relieving, or valuable is coming.
That’s why the best ads don’t just deliver info — they create emotional anticipation. You’re not
selling a product. You’re selling the next moment of dopamine.

🧠 Why dopamine matters in ads:
●​ It drives attention, engagement, and desire​
●​ It rewards the viewer for staying tuned in​
●​ It creates cravings that pull people toward the CTA​

✅ How to spike dopamine in your ad:
1.​ Introduce tension, contradiction, or surprise early​
2.​ Open a loop that hints at something transformational​
3.​ Use movement, novelty, or strong emotion in the first 3 seconds​

💬 Example triggers:
●​ “I didn’t expect this to work — but it changed everything.”​
●​ “What she says at 0:09 still gives me chills.”​
●​ “Most people never notice this… until it’s too late.”​

🎯 Dopamine-driven ad arc:
1.​ Curiosity rises → dopamine spikes​
2.​ Viewer watches → emotional investment grows​
3.​ Payoff lands → satisfaction (or relief)​
4.​ CTA hits → action feels natural and urgent

2.14 Hook Psychology — How to Trigger Dopamine and Attention
Hooks are not just about grabbing attention. They are neurological doorways that:
●​ Spike dopamine (novelty + reward anticipation)​
●​ Engage System 1 (emotion, pattern recognition)​
●​ Trigger tension (incomplete story, problem, shock)​

Hooks That Spike Attention:
Type

Description

Example Hook

Open Loop

Creates curiosity by starting but not
finishing a story

“This one thing ruined my life…
until it didn’t.”

Identity Trigger

Calls out a specific audience or
belief

“If you’re over 40 and can’t lose
weight — watch.”

Contradiction

Shows something that doesn’t
match expectations

“I cured anxiety with a $2 solution
from CVS.”

Pain Mirror

Reflects deep emotional pain back
to the viewer

“I used to cry every time I saw
myself in the mirror.”

Visual Pattern
Break

Unusual edit, zoom, freeze, meme
layout

Split screen, meme edit, glitch
transition

What Hooks Must Do:
●​ Create tension (via mystery, contradiction, or challenge)​
●​ Resonate with a desire or pain (emotional relevance)​
●​ Deliver a mini dopamine hit (novelty or promised relief)​

✅ The most effective hooks create a question in the viewer’s mind — and the only way to
answer it… is to keep watching.

​

🧠 Ads Are Part of the Whole Funnel — Not Standalone
​

Weapons:​
​

This is one of the most important mindset shifts most creatives strategists, and brand owners
need to make:
An ad is not your entire system. It’s a trigger inside your ecosystem.
Every ad you create sits somewhere within a broader funnel — and its performance depends on
understanding its role in that journey.
●​ A raw, problem-aware image ad doesn’t close the sale — it earns the click to your
landing page.​

●​ A solution-aware UGC doesn’t convert cold — it reinforces belief mid-funnel.​
●​ An ugly ad might not sell a $200 product — but it opens curiosity that drives the first
step.​

🎯 Your ad’s job is not to do everything. It’s to move the prospect forward one step.
That’s it.

You need to pair:
●​ The ad → gets attention and opens a loop​
●​ The landing page → builds desire and trust​
●​ The checkout → closes the deal with urgency and frictionless flow​

✅ Before judging an ad, always ask:
●​ Where is this in the funnel?​
●​ What level of awareness is the viewer at?​
●​ What does this ad actually need to accomplish?​

Because when you stop treating ads like solo players and start designing them as part of a
synchronized funnel, your entire system converts better — and your creatives stop carrying
weight they were never meant to hold.

🔄 The Psychology of Ugly Image Ads — And Why They
Break the Scroll

“Ugly” ads — those raw, meme-like, pixelated, badly cropped images — often outperform
polished creative. But why?
It’s not random. It’s psychological warfare against market sophistication.
Ugly image ads work because they feel native, unpredictable, and unfiltered. They
don’t trigger ad defenses. They feel like something a real person made.

🧠 Why They Work:
●​ They disrupt pattern recognition — the brain expects polished ads and scrolls right
past them. An ugly image forces a second look.​
●​ They create novelty — and novelty is one of the fastest ways to spike dopamine and
regain attention.​
●​ They lower resistance — because the viewer doesn’t realize it’s an ad right away.​

They scream “not a brand,” which makes people stop and investigate. They feel real, weird,
raw — and that’s exactly why they work.

⚠️ But here’s the truth:
Ugly image ads don’t sell alone. They’re entry points — not closers.
They’re designed to spike curiosity, not build belief.

✅ They need a strong landing page to do the heavy lifting:
●​ Educate​
●​ Build trust​
●​ Prove credibility​
●​ Close the sale​

So when you launch an ugly ad and it gets clicks but not sales — it’s not always the ad’s fault. It
did its job: it got attention. But if your LP is weak, confusing, or doesn’t match the vibe of the ad
— you lose them.
Treat ugly ads like doors. Your funnel still has to walk them through the house.

📈 Ad Creation Tips: How to Make Ads That Convert
(Without Relying on Fluff)

1. Speak Like a Real Person — Not a Marketer
People aren’t fooled by marketing language.​
They’re looking for clarity, not cleverness.

✅ Use:\

●​ Straightforward language​
●​ Clear structure​
●​ Tone that respects their intelligence​

🧠 If your script sounds like you’re “selling,” it’s too much.
2. Get to the Point — Fast
You have 1.5 seconds to earn attention.​
So stop trying to build suspense.​
Start with what matters:

✅ What’s the problem?​
✅ What’s the fix?​
✅ Why does this work better?

3. Your Ad Is a Trigger — Not a Sales Page
An ad isn’t supposed to close. It’s supposed to move someone one step forward.​
If your ad is trying to educate, close, and pitch all in one — you’ve lost already.

✅ Match your creative to your funnel stage:
●​ TOF = spark interest​
●​ MOF = build clarity and trust​

●​ BOF = eliminate hesitation​

4. Use Proof — Not Promises
Don’t say “it works.”​
Show it. Or let someone else say it.

✅ Before/afters​
✅ Screenshots​
✅ Quotes from real people​
✅ “I’ve tried X, Y, Z — this was the only one that…”​
That’s how people validate, compare, and buy.

5. One Point Per Frame. No Exceptions.
Most bad ads try to say too much at once.
●​ Text on screen + voiceover + cutaway demo + music = brain freeze​
●​ Clean visual + 1 sentence = watchable​

✅ If someone has to decode your ad, they’ll skip it.
6. Repeat the Offer — But Not Like a Robot
If your offer is good, it deserves to be repeated.​
But rephrase it. Show it from different angles.​
Make sure it’s understood, not just said.

✅ "Under $2/day"​
✅ "Less than your coffee"​
✅ "No refills, no subscriptions"

7. Don’t Create in Chunks — Flow Matters

If you write your hook on Monday… your body on Tuesday… and the CTA on Thursday after 12
distractions… it will read like Frankenstein.

✅ Block time. Write the full ad in one sitting.​
✅ Then edit it with a clear mind.​
✅ Make sure it flows start to finish.

8. Test Structured Variations — Not Random Ideas
Stop throwing spaghetti at the wall.​
Start running controlled experiments:
●​ Hook A vs. Hook B (same body)​
●​ Voiceover vs. no VO​
●​ Before/after first vs. proof first​

Creative testing is strategic — not spray and pray.

9. Keep the CTA Logical — Not Forceful
Your call-to-action should sound like the next logical step, not a desperate pitch.

✅ Good: “See how it works” / “Try it yourself”​
🚫 Bad: “Don’t miss out! Order now before it’s too late!”
You're not here to beg. You're here to guide.

10. It’s Not About You. It’s About Clarity.
Nobody cares if it’s your best idea.​
If the message isn’t instantly clear to someone who’s tired, distracted, or skeptical — it won’t
convert.

✅ Ask: “Would I understand this if I didn’t care?”​
✅ If no — delete, simplify, or reframe

📌 Final Note: Don’t Overthink — Build, Ship, Improve
These principles aren’t here so you stress Mfer about checking every box in one ad. They’re
here to sharpen your thinking while you write, create, or review ads — so you know what
actually matters.

👉 You’re not here to make the perfect ad. You’re here to make a lot of ads — and let the
market decide what wins.

🎯 The real goal is: Volume × Quality. Not just volume. Not just quality.
●​ Volume alone means you’ll pump out 10,000 pieces of junk with no progress.
●​ Quality alone means you’ll overthink, under-test, and get stuck chasing perfection.
You don’t get better by guessing. You get better by building, testing, learning, and repeating.

✅ The market decides the winner — not you. So create relentlessly. Improve strategically. And
keep moving MFERS

🔍 3. Image Ad Breakdown – First
Day IMAGE AD #3 :
​
https://app.tryatria.com/ad/m3229668260499723

​

Core Psychological Principles at Play in the Image
System 1 Thinking (Fast, Emotional, Intuitive):
●​ Hook & Emotional Relevance: The giant, bold headline "ARE YOU ALWAYS
SLEEPY?" in all caps is a powerful System 1 hook. It bypasses rational thought and
instantly targets a visceral, common pain point. The viewer immediately self-identifies or
ignores the ad.
●​ Visual Pattern Interrupt: The bright yellow background is an aggressive scroll-stopper.
It acts as a visual jolt designed to grab attention.
●​ Processing Fluency (Visual): The layout is clean, text is high contrast, and emojis are
used to make the headline and key vitamin list extremely easy to understand. The brain
doesn’t have to work hard to comprehend it.
System 2 Thinking (Slow, Logical, Deliberative) – Initiated:
●​ Problem Framing & Explanation: The subheadline "3 vitamin deficiencies can create
chemical imbalances that may cause exhaustion" starts to engage System 2. It
introduces a plausible (though simplified) cause for the System 1 pain.

●​ Specificity & Credibility (Initial): Mentioning "Vitamin D," "Vitamin B12," and
"Magnesium" adds logic and specificity. This makes the problem and potential solution
feel more concrete.
●​ Product as Solution: A centered product image surrounded by healthy fruits/vegetables
builds the idea that this is a natural and credible remedy. System 1 says
"healthy/natural," while System 2 sees it as a formulated solution.
Zeigarnik Effect (Incompleteness & Curiosity):
●​ The headline "ARE YOU ALWAYS SLEEPY?" opens a mental loop: “Am I?” and “Why?”
●​ The subheadline partially answers the why, and then the image implies the product is the
missing piece.
Loss Aversion (Implied):
●​ The phrase "ALWAYS SLEEPY" suggests loss of energy, productivity, and life
enjoyment. This taps into the discomfort of staying in a negative state.
Dopamine Spike (Novelty & Potential Reward):
●​ Bold question + bright color = pattern interrupt.
●​ The simple promise of a root cause and a possible fix (vitamin deficiencies + product)
triggers hope — a small dopamine hit.

How the Image Ad Works (Its Mechanism)
Interrupt & Qualify
●​ The headline and yellow background stop the scroll and quickly qualify the audience: “Is
this ad for me?”
Problem Agitation & Simplification
●​ It highlights a pain point (exhaustion) and immediately offers a simple explanation (3
vitamin deficiencies).
Introduce a Tangible Solution
●​ The product is presented in a clean, centered way with fresh foods implying health and
trust.
Prime for More Information
●​ The ad creates enough curiosity to make someone want to click. It gives a taste — not a
full picture.

Role in the Whole Funnel
Top of Funnel (TOF) – Primary Role
●​ Awareness & Attention: The ad grabs attention from tired people who may not know
why they feel that way.
●​ Problem Awareness/Education: It educates by introducing vitamin deficiency as a root
cause.
●​ Interest Generation: Curiosity is built toward the First Day product.
Early Middle of Funnel (MOF) – Secondary Role
●​ Solution Introduction: It positions First Day as a possible fix.
●​ Driving to Deeper Engagement: Encourages the user to click through to a landing
page for further logic-building (ingredients, proof, testimonials).

What the Image Ad Doesn't Do (and Isn’t Designed To):
●​ Deep System 2 Conviction: It only hints at a logical solution. Full persuasion is deferred
to the landing page.
●​ Overcome Major Skepticism: Lacks deep proof, testimonials, or detailed research.
●​ Social Proof: No review count, before/after, or community shown.
●​ Hard Sell/Direct Conversion: No price or CTA button. Implied CTA is "Learn More."

Conclusion
This image acts as a highly efficient scroll-stopper and problem-qualifier. It activates strong
System 1 triggers (emotion, visual impact) while lightly engaging System 2 with a simplified
logical narrative. Its ultimate goal is to move the viewer further into the funnel where deeper
persuasion happens.​
​
LP: https://discover.firstday.com/wds-sales-exhaustion-lp
In summary:​
The landing page is a logical and necessary continuation of the journey started by the static
image ad.​
●​ The ad hooks them with a problem and a hint of a solution.

●​ The LP reels them in by confirming their problem, educating them thoroughly, building
overwhelming social proof, and making a compelling, risk-free offer.

🔍 4. Ugly Image Ad Breakdown – IMAGE AD #4 :
https://app.tryatria.com/ad/m560395226780496

Is This an "Ugly Image Ad"?
Yes – with caveats. It strongly leans into the psychology of ugly ads, though it’s not overtly low
quality or meme-like.
●​ Disrupts Pattern Recognition: The inflamed, textured red area on the body is visually
unsettling. Not a polished, lifestyle ad – it feels clinical or even alarming.
●​ Creates Novelty & Curiosity: The phrase "Cortisol Poisoning" is new and dramatic. The
unfamiliar visual invites inspection.
●​ Lowers Ad Defenses: Doesn’t feel like a traditional ad at first glance — more like a
medical infographic or warning. This bypasses ad fatigue.
●​ Feels "Real" or "Serious": The visual resembles something you’d see in a medical
presentation, creating urgency and realism.
What it’s NOT (Classic Ugly Ad Traits):
●​ Not pixelated, poorly cropped, or chaotic.
●​ No overuse of emojis or random overlays.

●​ Still structured and clean.
Conclusion: It employs ugly ad psychology — disruption, novelty, lowered defenses — with a
clinical/medical design twist. Less “chaotic meme,” more “diabolical infographic.”

Core Psychological Principles at Play in the Image & Copy
System 1 Thinking (Fast, Emotional, Intuitive)
●​ Hook & Emotional Relevance: "The Most Misunderstood Menopause Symptom:
Cortisol Poisoning" is dramatic, identity-targeted, and fear-inducing.
○​ "Misunderstood" = intrigue, insider knowledge.
○​ "Menopause Symptom" = demographic callout.
○​ "Cortisol Poisoning" = invented fear trigger.
●​ Visual Alarm: The red inflamed spot evokes concern. Arrows amplify focus. Viewer
feels “this looks bad – what is it?”
●​ Fear-Based Copy:
○​ "Cortisol might be eating your hip tendons like termites"
○​ "Rogue hormone dismantling your body piece by piece"
○​ "Silent collagen killer"
○​ These phrases emotionally hijack the viewer’s attention and create urgency.
System 2 Thinking (Slow, Logical, Deliberative)
●​ Problem Framing & Pseudo-Scientific Justification: Reframes joint pain, fatigue,
aging skin as effects of a misunderstood cause ("Cortisol Poisoning").
●​ Borrowed Authority: Mentions studies from University of California, King’s College
London, UC San Diego — increases believability.
●​ Specificity in Copy:
○​ "Reduce morning cortisol levels by 29%"
○​ "Improved joint mobility by 41%"
○​ These stats create perceived credibility and logic.
Zeigarnik Effect (Open Loop)
●​ Headline: "The Most Misunderstood Symptom..." prompts: “Why is it misunderstood?
What is cortisol poisoning?”
●​ Copy continues loop: “Doctors miss it 83% of the time.”
Loss Aversion (Dominant Principle)
●​ Visual: Red, inflamed organ suggests damage/loss.
●​ Copy: Drips with threats of ongoing or future loss:
○​ "Sagging skin that ages 10 years"

○​ "Radiating hip pain"
○​ "Sleep disruption"
○​ "Collagen breakdown"
○​ "Mobility loss"
●​ Framing: If you don’t act now, you lose youth, comfort, beauty, sleep.
Anticipated Regret
●​ Narrative: Doctors miss this. If you ignore this now, you’ll regret it later.
●​ Implied Message: “What if your doctor is wrong?”
Dopamine Spike (Fear, Novelty, and Hope)
●​ Novelty: Unheard term “Cortisol Poisoning.”
●​ Fear/Surprise: Alarming symptoms.
●​ Hope: "Provitalize" = the natural solution that fixes everything.
Identity Trigger (Explicit)
●​ Copy: “Engineered specifically for women over 50 with hip and joint pain.”
●​ Clear callout of the exact avatar. Viewer feels: "They’re talking to me."

How the Image Ad Works (Its Mechanism)
Shock & Awe Hook
●​ Visual + term “Cortisol Poisoning” = Scroll-breaking.
●​ Feels urgent, unfamiliar, medical.
Problem Reframing & Amplification
●​ Takes familiar menopause symptoms and reframes them under a new, sinister lens.
●​ Then heightens concern via extreme metaphors (“eating your tendons like termites”).

●​

"Doctors miss it 83% of the time."
"Your doctor won’t tell you this."
"Big Pharma can’t patent natural solutions."
Destroys trust in conventional wisdom, primes user to believe a new solution.

Introduce Secret Solution
●​ Provitalize is the answer.
●​ Framed as natural, safe, and science-backed.

Drive to Click
●​ Image is built to create discomfort and curiosity.
●​ Copy immediately delivers high agitation + solution, priming for a landing page visit.

Role in the Funnel
Top of Funnel (TOF)
●​ Purpose: Attention + curiosity + fear.
●​ Targets: Women 50+ with menopause-related pain/fatigue.
●​ Reframes: Offers a new way to view their symptoms.
MOF Transition Within Copy
●​ Copy immediately builds:
○​ Product credibility (studies, natural ingredients).
○​ Social proof (testimonials).
○​ Offer and guarantee.
●​ Aims to accelerate from TOF to MOF conversion without leaving the ad.

Summary
This is a classic TOF “ugly ad” in disguise:
●​ Image = scroll-breaking, fear-inducing, curiosity-generating.
●​ Headline = massive open loop and identity trigger.
●​ Copy = mini-sales letter: problem agitation, skepticism toward mainstream, solution
intro, proof, and soft CTA.
●​ Designed for cold traffic and first contact — the image stops the scroll, the copy pulls
them into the story, and the link drives them to the BOF-heavy landing page for final

FILE: The Ultimate SOP for Creating Killer Ads By Zakaria_Airakaz.txt

The Ultimate SOP for Creating Killer Ads
By Zakaria_Airakaz

1. �� The Mission & Philosophy of Killer Ads
The purpose of a high-converting ad is not to convince — it’s to align belief and trigger
resonance.
A great ad:
● Captures attention through emotional relevance
● Builds belief through logic and story
● Makes conversion feel inevitable and self-driven

Ads are belief engineering systems.
They are not designed to push a product — they are built to resolve emotional tension in your
avatar’s life.

2. �� Core Psychological Frameworks That Drive
Ad Success
Below are the deepest psychological levers used to construct and diagnose ad performance
across all formats (UGC, VSLs, Static).

2.1 System 1 & 2 Thinking – Daniel Kahneman
This foundational framework explains how the brain makes decisions, and it's critical for
structuring winning ads. ( I already i explained this one on the LP SOP here is a reminder)
Note: This principle is crucial because it aligns with how humans naturally think and make
decisions. The key is understanding which system — emotional (System 1) or rational
(System

2) — dominates in your specific avatar. Only then can you craft messaging that truly resonates
and drives action.
EG:

System 1-Dominant Avatar
Example: Woman dealing with PCOS-related hair loss
● Feels hopeless, ashamed, and emotionally drained from failed fixes
● Makes decisions based on how a story feels, not what it proves
● Needs to feel understood, not educated

System 2-Dominant Avatar
Example: 40+ German Mfer man with chronic back pain
● Logical, skeptical, wants clinical proof and long-term relief
● Compares options, reads reviews, checks mechanisms
● Needs explanation before belief
System 1: The Fast, Feeling Brain
● Instinctive, subconscious, emotional, fast.
● Processes visuals, emotions, patterns, and stories in milliseconds.
● It decides before the conscious brain catches up.
�� In Ads:
● Your hook must target System 1.
● Use facial expressions, contrast, emotional mirroring, and pattern interruption.
● Visuals > words. Stories > stats. Emotion > explanation.
�� Example:
● Showing a woman crying in front of a mirror (System 1) will outperform a line that says
"Improve your skin clarity" (System 2).
System 2: The Slow, Thinking Brain
● Analytical, conscious, logical, effortful.

● Kicks in after interest is sparked.
● It justifies, compares, evaluates.
�� In Ads:
● Your ad must shift gears after the emotional hook.
● Present your unique mechanism, customer proof, scientific backing.
● This is where the belief is confirmed.
�� Example:
● After showing the emotional struggle, explain: "This device uses red light at a
wavelength of 660nm, clinically shown to increase collagen by 31% in 8 weeks."
�� High-performing ads work because they mimic how the brain works:
1. System 1: "This feels like me. I’m in."
2. System 2: "This makes sense. I trust it."
If you skip System 1: You never get attention. If you skip System 2: You never get
conversion.

➡️ Structure your ads like this (System 1 → System 2 flow):

● 0–3s: Visual & emotional hook (System 1)
● 4–10s: Avatar pain + agitation of the problem (System 1)
● 11–18s: Mechanism, proof, and logical solution (System 2)
● 19–24s: Paint the desire — what life looks like after using the product (System 2 +
emotional closure)
● 25s+: Clear, low-friction, benefit-driven CTA
The secret to high ROAS isn’t better logic — it’s starting with better emotion.

2.2 COM-B Model – Behavior Change Science
The COM-B model explains what’s truly required for someone to take action. It stands for:
● Capability – “Can I do this?”
● Opportunity – “Is this possible for me right now?”
● Motivation – “Do I even want this badly enough?”

Without all three present, action won't happen.
�� Capability (C)
The person must believe they’re capable of achieving the result.
● Is the product easy to use?
● Does it feel like something they can actually do?
�� In Ads:
● Show the product in use by someone like them
● Use language like: “It’s as simple as...” or “Anyone can follow this.”
�� Example:
● “Even if you’ve failed 5 times before — this takes 90 seconds a day.”
�� Opportunity (O)
They must have the external chance to do it.
● Is the product available?
● Is it affordable? Can they get it in their country? Is it in stock?
�� In Ads:
● Show clear pricing, shipping info, availability
● Use phrases like: “Ships today,” “In stock now,” “Under $1/day.”
�� Example:
● “Delivered to your door. No prescription needed.”
�� Motivation (M)
They must truly desire the outcome your product provides.
● Is the emotional or functional result powerful enough? ●
Are they personally invested in fixing it?
�� In Ads:
● Mirror their internal frustration or goal
● Paint a clear before/after transformation

● Use emotional language: “You deserve to feel like yourself again.”
�� Example:
● “You’ve tried everything — but this time, the results stay.”
�� Use the COM-B Model to diagnose friction:
● If an ad isn’t converting, ask:
○ Do they believe they can do this? (C)
○ Is it clearly available to them? (O)
○ Is the desire powerful and emotional enough? (M)

✅ The best ads remove friction in all 3 — making action feel natural, obvious, and urgent.
2.3 Self-Determination Theory ( This is the reason I keep saying stick to one avatar and
try to know him the best )
This theory explains the 3 fundamental psychological needs that must be satisfied for a person
to feel empowered enough to act — especially when the action involves change, purchase, or
commitment.

⚙️ Autonomy – "I choose this myself"

People resist being sold to. They want to feel like the decision was theirs — not the result of
pressure.
�� In Ads:
● Let the viewer feel in control
● Use copy that invites rather than pushes
● Avoid heavy-handed CTAs
�� Example:
● “Here’s what finally worked for me. You decide if it’s right for you.”
�� Competence – "I can actually do this"
If something feels too hard, confusing, or uncertain — people avoid it. Belief in their ability to
succeed is essential.
�� In Ads:
● Use simple demos or testimonials showing success by people like them ●

Reduce perceived effort or learning curve
�� Example:
● “I was skeptical too — but all I had to do was use it for 3 minutes a day.”
�� Relatedness – "This is made for someone like me"
People want to feel seen and understood. They’re far more likely to take action when they feel
the brand “gets” their situation.
�� In Ads:
● Feature UGC or testimonials from relatable avatars
● Use language and visuals that reflect their real world
�� Example:
● “I’m a mom of 3 — this was the first thing that actually fit my routine.”
�� High-converting ads satisfy all 3:
● Autonomy: Viewer feels in control, not pushed
● Competence: Viewer believes they can succeed
● Relatedness: Viewer feels emotionally safe and understood

➡️ When these 3 align, resistance disappears — and the viewer moves forward because they
want to, not because they’re being sold.

2.4 Narrative Transportation Theory ( this why I like VSLs or UGC vsl style stories
always dominate)
We act when we’re emotionally transported into a story — not just shown a product.
Stories create immersion. When a viewer gets pulled into a narrative, they temporarily suspend
skepticism and emotionally live the transformation.
This is called "narrative transportation." And it creates a high-trust, low-resistance
state.
�� Why it works:
● The brain processes stories like experiences, not facts.
● When someone watches a relatable transformation, mirror neurons activate — making
them feel like it's happening to them. And Mfers if it is done right, this creates a

trance-like emotional state where the prospect doesn’t just want the result… they feel
like they’ve already experienced it. And that emotional imprint drives a powerful urge to
take action and own the product.
● This emotional engagement increases attention span, memory retention, and
conversion.

✅ Great ads are micro-stories — not pitches.
Structure of a Story-Driven Ad:

1. Problem: Start with the avatar's emotional pain or frustration.
2. Tension: Show the struggle, the breaking point, or the moment of doubt. 3.
Solution: Introduce the product naturally — not as a savior, but as the enabler of
change.
4. Resolution: Paint what life looks like after — the relief, pride, control, or peace.
�� Example:
● “I used to dread leaving the house. Now, I go out feeling confident again.”

✅ Story-driven ads work best with:
● Raw UGCs
● Hero story VSLs

�� Use stories that reflect your viewer’s starting point — and take them through a
believable, emotionally satisfying transformation.
Because at the end of the day, people don’t remember what you told them. They remember how
you made them feel.

2.5 Loss Aversion (Behavioral Economics)
Loss Aversion is one of the most proven psychological principles in behavioral economics. It
shows that people feel the pain of a loss twice as strongly as the pleasure of a gain.
In other words, your prospect is more likely to take action to avoid something bad
than to chase something good.
This principle flips the default persuasion strategy. Instead of only saying “here’s what you’ll
get,” you must emphasize, “here’s what you’re losing if you don’t act.”
�� Why it works:

● The human brain is wired for survival. Avoiding loss feels urgent. Again Mfers most us
don’t change our life chasing a dream. We change because not changing becomes too
painful. When there’s too much to lose, fear takes the wheel — and that’s what drives
action. It’s not the outcome that pushes us— it’s the cost of staying the same.
● Loss-based framing taps into fear, anxiety, and discomfort — which are powerful
motivators for decision-making.

✅ Great ads don’t just highlight benefits — they shine a spotlight on the cost of doing nothing.
�� Example:
● “Every day you wait, your hair keeps thinning — and your beauty keeps slipping.” ●
“Ignore the back pain, and in six months, you’ll be looking at surgery instead of relief.”
�� How to apply Loss Aversion in Ads:
● Show what they’re currently losing (health, time, joy, confidence)
● Frame the problem as getting worse with inaction
● Make “waiting” feel like the dangerous option
�� CTA Twist: Instead of:
● “Buy now to feel better” Use:
● “Stop letting this steal your energy and confidence”

✅ When your ad makes the viewer feel like they’re already losing, the urgency to act becomes
natural — and conversion follows.

2.6 Anticipated Regret
Anticipated regret is a powerful psychological driver — rooted in our fear of looking back and
wishing we had acted sooner.
People are often more motivated to avoid future guilt than to chase current gain.
The emotional pain of “What if I had...” can be so vivid, it compels action now.

✅ Great ads activate this future pain in the present — turning hesitation into urgency.
�� Why it works:

● We visualize our future self regretting inaction more vividly than enjoying success.
● Fear of missing the window to act creates real-time tension.
● This works especially well for health, appearance, opportunity, and time-sensitive offers.
�� Example angles:
● “You’ll wish you had started 3 months ago.”
● “This might be your last shot before it gets worse.”
● “Fix it now, or fix something bigger later.”
�� In Ads:
● Use future pacing: “Imagine where you’ll be 6 months from now…”
● Layer testimonials that express regret: “I wish I hadn’t waited so long to try this.” ●
Include soft deadline pressure: “Thousands have already started — what’s stopping
you?”

✅ When your ad plants the seed of future disappointment, you create urgency that feels
internal — not forced.

I know some of you Mfers will get confused What is the difference between Loss Aversion and
Anticipated Regret:
● Loss Aversion = “I don’t want to lose what I have now.”
○ Focus: Present pain or risk
○ Trigger: Fear of losing something you already possess (health, time, money,
confidence)
○ Emotional Driver: Avoiding a current or ongoing loss
● Anticipated Regret = “I’ll regret it later if I don’t act now.”
○ Focus: Future guilt or missed opportunity
○ Trigger: Fear of looking back and wishing they made a different choice
○ Emotional Driver: Avoiding future pain or what-if

2.7 Mirror Neurons & Identification
Mirror neurons are brain cells that fire not only when we do something — but also when we

observe someone else doing it. This is why we can feel pain, joy, or relief just by watching
someone go through it — our brain is mirroring the emotional experience.
In advertising, this means if your viewer watches someone like them win, they
start to emotionally experience that win too.
This creates belief, emotional resonance, and a surge in motivation — because it stops being
their story and becomes mine.
�� Why this matters:
When your ad reflects your audience’s reality — their fears, hopes, frustrations, language — it
dissolves resistance and builds trust fast. They’re not watching an ad. They’re watching
themselves.

✅ Ads must show avatars that reflect your audience’s:

● Life stage — age, gender, daily role (e.g., young mom, working dad, retiree)
● Emotional pain — shame, fear, doubt, confusion, regret
● Social context — how they live, speak, behave, dress, scroll, interact
�� Example:
● “I’m a 32-year-old mom who’s been battling PCOS for years — and I was terrified of
losing more hair… until I tried this.”
�� In Ads:
● Use real customers or relatable actors with the same body type, language, lifestyle
● Prioritize native settings: a bathroom mirror, the car, the living room
● Let them see themselves in the outcome
Because when a viewer sees someone like them transform, the brain doesn’t say: “I want that.”
It says: “That’s possible for me too.”
I know some of you Mfers will get confused What is the difference between Mirror Neurons &
Identification and Self-Determination Theory:
● Mirror Neurons & Identification = “That’s me.”
○ Mechanism: Neurological mirroring
○ Trigger: Watching someone like us succeed or struggle
○ Effect: We feel their experience as if it's ours
○ Purpose in ads: Create emotional resonance and visual recognition
● Self-Determination Theory = “I want this because I choose it.”
○ Mechanism: Psychological motivation model

○ 3 Core Needs:
■ Autonomy – “I choose this freely”
■ Competence – “I believe I can do this”
■ Relatedness – “This fits someone like me”
○ Effect: The viewer feels emotionally safe, capable, and motivated
○ Purpose in ads: Reduce internal resistance and trigger self-motivated action

2.8 Processing Fluency
Processing Fluency is the psychological principle that explains how the brain favors ease. When
something is easy to see, read, or understand, it feels more true, trustworthy, and desirable.
If it takes effort to understand, the brain interprets it as risky, confusing,
or untrustworthy.
This applies to both the visual structure and verbal delivery in your ads.
�� Why this matters in advertising:
● The average viewer makes a judgment in under 1 second
● Mental effort = friction, and friction kills conversions
● When your ad flows smoothly, the brain says: “This makes sense.” And this is your
biggest issue MFERS this time MFERS with capital �� — you write your ads in pieces.
You come up with the hook, then go scroll on Instagram, talk on Discord, and later come
back to slap the rest of it together. Then you send it over like, “Yo, can you review this?”
Bro, you're killing my brain cells. The flow’s completely off because you didn’t write it in
one focused block. You didn’t sit down and stay in the zone to make sure it reads
smooth, front to back. At the very least, you need to set time to write in one shot — or
give it a clean pass to ensure it actually feels like one cohesive message.

✅ High-converting ads feel simple and intuitive — even if the message is deep
Visual Processing Fluency:

● Avoid visual clutter (too much text, chaotic backgrounds): Now I know you’re already
thinking, “But what about those f*cking TikTok ads with a million cuts and chaos
everywhere? read this Mfers with my trolling voice:

Those TikTok-style ads with fast transitions, flashy B-rolls, and jump cuts do work — but
not because they’re chaotic. They work because they’re designed for attention-deficit
environments where every second is a battle to stay on screen.

✅

But here’s what most people miss:
The best of those “chaotic” ads still have fluency underneath the noise.
● Each scene has one clear message.
● Text is bold and bite-sized.
● Visuals follow a rhythm — even if it’s fast.
● Every transition is designed to reset attention without confusing the viewer.
So it’s not about clutter — it’s about controlled chaos with clarity at its core. Think
of it like this:
It’s loud, fast, and aggressive — but it’s intentional and fluent.
If your ad feels like a jumbled mess or jumps topic mid-frame — it’s not a
TikTok-style win, it’s just trash and if you keep sending me trash I will leak
your store and make people make fun of you so level your game up Mfers

● Use contrast and white space to guide the eye
● Stick to one focal point per frame
�� Example:
● Before: 3 text blocks, no spacing, no hierarchy
● After: One bold headline, one line of benefit, clear CTA
Verbal Processing Fluency:
● Use short words, short sentences
● Avoid jargon or abstract metaphors
● Speak like the customer speaks
�� Example:
● Instead of: “A revolutionary, full-spectrum, biophotonic rejuvenation system…”
● Say: “Red light that helps your skin look younger — fast.”

✅ Keep your ads clear, clean, and conversational. If a 12-year-old can’t understand it in 3
seconds, simplify it.

Because in ads, confusion doesn’t cause curiosity — it causes exits.

2.9 Anchoring
Anchoring is a pricing psychology principle that shows how our brains don’t evaluate price in
isolation — we compare it to the first number we see.
That first number becomes the "anchor," and it sets the baseline for what we think
something should cost.

⚠️ Important Distinction:

Anchoring is primarily an offer strategy, not a top-of-funnel ad tactic. But it can still be very
effective inside ads when used at the right time.

✅ Best used in ads when:

● You're retargeting warm traffic or cart abandoners basically MOF/BOF ● You're
running a longer VSL where you frame price/value at the end ● You're using UGC
where the buyer talks about how surprisingly affordable it was
�� Not ideal for cold ads: TOF
● Anchoring too early can break fluency and feel like a hard pitch
● Most cold viewers aren’t ready to hear about price — they need belief and emotion first

�� Why Anchoring Works:
● Our brains need a reference point to decide what’s “expensive” or “affordable”
● The initial number shown influences everything after it — even if it's irrelevant

✅ In the right kind of ad, use:

● A higher price first, then reveal your actual offer
● Side-by-side comparisons or slashed pricing
�� Examples:
● “Normally $299 — today only $79.”
● “Most people spend over $1,200 on treatments that barely touch the root cause. This
does more — for less than a week’s worth of takeout.”

● “A single Botox session costs $350 — and wears off in months. This helps rebuild your
collagen naturally for under $2/day. No needles. No regret.”
● “Physical therapy: $85/session. Massage: $120/hr. Back surgery? Over $18,000.This?
One-time $179 — and it sits in your drawer, ready whenever you need relief.”
�� Anchoring can also apply to value perception:
● “10+ hours of relief in one 3-minute session.”
● “$1/day to finally sleep through the night.”

✅ Use it carefully and strategically — anchoring works best when your viewer is already sold
emotionally and just needs a logical reason to act now. that shows how our brains don’t
evaluate price in isolation — we compare it to the first number we see.

That first number becomes the "anchor," and it sets the baseline for what we think
something should cost.
This is why luxury brands always show the highest price first — and why discounts feel way
more powerful when we see what we’re “saving” versus what we’re paying.

2.10 The Pratfall Effect
The Pratfall Effect is a psychological principle showing that people actually trust and like you
more when you show a small, harmless flaw — as long as you’re otherwise competent. In
advertising, this means imperfection isn’t a liability. It’s a tool. This principle is also what makes
ugly ads work so well. When you see a content creator filming in a messy house, with regular
lighting, home clothes, unbrushed hair — it hits different. It’s not a promotion. It’s not polished.
It’s real. You’re watching a normal human being who’s showing up raw, flaws and all — and
that’s exactly why we trust them.
A little mess makes your message feel real.
When your ads are too polished or overly scripted, they lose believability. But when they show
human quirks — like a stutter, a laugh, or a slightly chaotic background — they feel authentic
and relatable. Viewers don’t just watch, they lean in.
�� Why this works:
● Perfection triggers skepticism
● Flaws feel honest, and honesty builds trust
● Imperfection lowers resistance by mirroring real life

✅ In Ads:
● Show unfiltered UGCs with messy hair, pauses, or raw moments
● Keep background noise, small mistakes, and casual delivery
● Avoid overediting or stiff scripting — let the human moments stay
�� Example:
● “Okay, ignore how tired I look — I’ve been using this for 3 weeks and it’s actually
helping.”
● Laughs mid-sentence “Wow I sound like an infomercial, but it really works.”

✅ Ads that use the Pratfall Effect feel like a recommendation from a friend — not a pitch from a
brand. That’s why they convert.

�� Don’t aim for perfection. Aim for connection.

2.11 The Zeigarnik Effect
The Zeigarnik Effect is the psychological tendency to remember unfinished tasks or
incomplete stories better than completed ones. In advertising, this becomes a powerful tool for
holding attention — because the brain hates unresolved tension.
If something starts but doesn’t finish, the mind stays hooked until it gets closure.
This is what I call the knowledge gap. Always create a gap in your viewer’s mind —
a space between what they know and what they need to know. That gap pulls
attention like a magnet and leads to longer watch time, every single time.

✅ That’s why your hook should open a loop — a question, a cliffhanger, or a surprising
statement that demands resolution.

�� Why it works:
● Incomplete information creates mental tension
● Curiosity becomes a discomfort the viewer wants to resolve
● The brain stays engaged to close the loop
�� Example Hooks:
● PROBLEM-AWARE :
○ “I thought my hormones were the problem… but the real reason my hair kept

falling out was way worse.”
“Every shower, I’d lose more — until one blood result finally explained
everything.”
○ “I tried biotin, oils, scalp brushes… none of it worked. And the worst part? I found
out why way too late.”
● SOLUTION-AWARE:
○ “I spent $600 on supplements before realizing the one hormone no one talks
about was ruining my progress.”
○ “It wasn’t a deficiency — it was something my doctor missed entirely.” ○ “I thought
I was doing everything right… until I tracked this one thing for a week.”

✅ In Ads:
● Don’t give away the result in the first frame
● Build story or curiosity before solution
● Let the first 5–10 seconds stir the question, not the pitch
�� Good Zeigarnik-style ads create an emotional itch the viewer has to scratch — and the
only way to do that is to watch until the end.

2.12 Social Proof & Bandwagon Bias
Social Proof is the psychological principle that we look to others when we’re unsure what to do
— and Bandwagon Bias says the more people we see doing something, the more likely we are
to join in.
When we don’t fully trust ourselves, we trust the crowd.
In advertising, this means showing that your product is already loved, trusted, and used by
others instantly builds trust and reduces buying resistance.
�� Why it works:
● Makes the viewer feel they’re not taking a risk
● Activates “safety in numbers”
● Creates FOMO (Fear of Missing Out) when everyone else seems to be using it

✅ How to apply in ads:

● Use review screenshots, quick montages of testimonials, or “reaction” duet content
● Highlight big metrics:
○ “Over 1 million sold”
○ “4.9 stars from 30,000+ customers”
○ “Trusted by 8,000 women with PCOS”
● Include comments, shares, or clips with people saying, “I saw this everywhere…”

�� Example lines:
● “I wasn’t sure at first, but with over 12,000 five-star reviews, I gave it a shot.”
● “Everyone I know is trying this — so I finally did too.”
● “It showed up in three of my group chats… that’s when I knew I had to try it.”

�� You’re not just selling a product. You’re selling the comfort of joining a movement
that already makes sense to everyone else.

2.13 The Psychology of Dopamine in Ads
Dopamine is the brain’s chemical of anticipation and reward — and it plays a massive role in
how viewers respond to your ad.
It’s not released when someone gets something — it spikes when they
expect something exciting, relieving, or valuable is coming.
That’s why the best ads don’t just deliver info — they create emotional anticipation. You’re not
selling a product. You’re selling the next moment of dopamine.

�� Why dopamine matters in ads:
● It drives attention, engagement, and desire

● It rewards the viewer for staying tuned in

✅ How to spike dopamine in your ad:

● It creates cravings that pull people toward the CTA

1. Introduce tension, contradiction, or surprise early
2. Open a loop that hints at something transformational
3. Use movement, novelty, or strong emotion in the first 3 seconds

�� Example triggers:
● “I didn’t expect this to work — but it changed everything.”
● “What she says at 0:09 still gives me chills.”
● “Most people never notice this… until it’s too late.”

�� Dopamine-driven ad arc:
1. Curiosity rises → dopamine spikes
2. Viewer watches → emotional investment grows
3. Payoff lands → satisfaction (or relief)
4. CTA hits → action feels natural and urgent

2.14 Hook Psychology — How to Trigger Dopamine and Attention
Hooks are not just about grabbing attention. They are neurological doorways that:
● Spike dopamine (novelty + reward anticipation)
● Engage System 1 (emotion, pattern recognition)
● Trigger tension (incomplete story, problem, shock)

Hooks That Spike Attention:
Type Description Example Hook

not finishing a story

What Hooks Must Do:
● Create tension (via mystery, contradiction, or challenge)
● Resonate with a desire or pain (emotional relevance)
● Deliver a mini dopamine hit (novelty or promised relief)

✅ The most effective hooks create a question in the viewer’s mind — and the only way to
answer it… is to keep watching.

�� Ads Are Part of the Whole Funnel — Not
Standalone Weapons:
This is one of the most important mindset shifts most creatives strategists, and brand owners
need to make:
An ad is not your entire system. It’s a trigger inside your ecosystem.
Every ad you create sits somewhere within a broader funnel — and its performance depends on
understanding its role in that journey.

● A raw, problem-aware image ad doesn’t close the sale — it earns the click to your
landing page.
● A solution-aware UGC doesn’t convert cold — it reinforces belief mid-funnel.
● An ugly ad might not sell a $200 product — but it opens curiosity that drives the first
step.

�� Your ad’s job is not to do everything. It’s to move the prospect forward one
step. That’s it.
You need to pair:
● The ad → gets attention and opens a loop
● The landing page → builds desire and trust
● The checkout → closes the deal with urgency and frictionless flow

✅ Before judging an ad, always ask:
● Where is this in the funnel?
● What level of awareness is the viewer at?
● What does this ad actually need to accomplish?

Because when you stop treating ads like solo players and start designing them as part of a
synchronized funnel, your entire system converts better — and your creatives stop carrying
weight they were never meant to hold.

�� The Psychology of Ugly Image Ads — And Why
They Break the Scroll
“Ugly” ads — those raw, meme-like, pixelated, badly cropped images — often outperform
polished creative. But why?
It’s not random. It’s psychological warfare against market sophistication.

Ugly image ads work because they feel native, unpredictable, and unfiltered. They
don’t trigger ad defenses. They feel like something a real person made.

�� Why They Work:
● They disrupt pattern recognition — the brain expects polished ads and scrolls right
past them. An ugly image forces a second look.
● They create novelty — and novelty is one of the fastest ways to spike dopamine and
regain attention.
● They lower resistance — because the viewer doesn’t realize it’s an ad right away.

They scream “not a brand,” which makes people stop and investigate. They feel real, weird,
raw — and that’s exactly why they work.

⚠️ But here’s the truth:
Ugly image ads don’t sell alone. They’re entry points — not closers.
They’re designed to spike curiosity, not build belief.

✅ They need a strong landing page to do the heavy lifting:
● Educate
● Build trust
● Prove credibility
● Close the sale

So when you launch an ugly ad and it gets clicks but not sales — it’s not always the ad’s fault. It
did its job: it got attention. But if your LP is weak, confusing, or doesn’t match the vibe of the ad
— you lose them.
Treat ugly ads like doors. Your funnel still has to walk them through the house.

�� Ad Creation Tips: How to Make Ads That

Convert (Without Relying on Fluff)
1. Speak Like a Real Person — Not a Marketer
People aren’t fooled by marketing language.
They’re looking for clarity, not cleverness.

✅ Use:\

● Straightforward language
● Clear structure
● Tone that respects their intelligence

�� If your script sounds like you’re “selling,” it’s too much.

2. Get to the Point — Fast
You have 1.5 seconds to earn attention.
So stop trying to build suspense.
Start with what matters:

✅ What’s the problem?
✅ What’s the fix?
✅ Why does this work better?

3. Your Ad Is a Trigger — Not a Sales Page
An ad isn’t supposed to close. It’s supposed to move someone one step forward.
If your ad is trying to educate, close, and pitch all in one — you’ve lost already.

✅ Match your creative to your funnel stage:
● TOF = spark interest
● MOF = build clarity and trust
● BOF = eliminate hesitation

4. Use Proof — Not Promises
Don’t say “it works.”
Show it. Or let someone else say it.

✅ Before/afters
✅ Screenshots
✅ Quotes from real people
✅ “I’ve tried X, Y, Z — this was the only one that…”
That’s how people validate, compare, and buy.

5. One Point Per Frame. No Exceptions.
Most bad ads try to say too much at once.
● Text on screen + voiceover + cutaway demo + music = brain freeze
● Clean visual + 1 sentence = watchable

✅ If someone has to decode your ad, they’ll skip it.
6. Repeat the Offer — But Not Like a Robot
If your offer is good, it deserves to be repeated.
But rephrase it. Show it from different angles.
Make sure it’s understood, not just said.

✅ "Under $2/day"
✅ "Less than your coffee"
✅ "No refills, no subscriptions"

7. Don’t Create in Chunks — Flow Matters
If you write your hook on Monday… your body on Tuesday… and the CTA on Thursday after 12
distractions… it will read like Frankenstein.

✅ Block time. Write the full ad in one sitting.
✅ Then edit it with a clear mind.
✅ Make sure it flows start to finish.
8. Test Structured Variations — Not Random Ideas
Stop throwing spaghetti at the wall.
Start running controlled experiments:
● Hook A vs. Hook B (same body)
● Voiceover vs. no VO
● Before/after first vs. proof first

Creative testing is strategic — not spray and pray.

9. Keep the CTA Logical — Not Forceful
Your call-to-action should sound like the next logical step, not a desperate pitch.

✅ Good: “See how it works” / “Try it yourself”

�� Bad: “Don’t miss out! Order now before it’s too late!”

You're not here to beg. You're here to guide.

10. It’s Not About You. It’s About Clarity.
Nobody cares if it’s your best idea.
If the message isn’t instantly clear to someone who’s tired, distracted, or skeptical — it
won’t convert.

✅ Ask: “Would I understand this if I didn’t care?”
✅ If no — delete, simplify, or reframe

�� Final Note: Don’t Overthink — Build, Ship, Improve
These principles aren’t here so you stress Mfer about checking every box in one ad. They’re

here to sharpen your thinking while you write, create, or review ads — so you know what
actually matters.
�� You’re not here to make the perfect ad. You’re here to make a lot of ads — and let
the market decide what wins.
�� The real goal is: Volume × Quality. Not just volume. Not just quality.
● Volume alone means you’ll pump out 10,000 pieces of junk with no progress. ●
Quality alone means you’ll overthink, under-test, and get stuck chasing perfection.
You don’t get better by guessing. You get better by building, testing, learning, and repeating.

✅ The market decides the winner — not you. So create relentlessly. Improve strategically. And
keep moving MFERS
